<h1 class="firstHeading mw-first-heading" id="firstHeading"><span class="mw-page-title-main">Chemical element</span></h1>
<div class="vector-body" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"></div>
<div id="contentSub2"></div>
<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#searchInput">Jump to search</a>
<div class="mw-body-content mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Species of atoms with a specific number of protons</div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:462px;"><a href="/wiki/Periodic_table" title="Periodic table"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="350" data-file-width="1096" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Periodic_table_%2832-col%2C_enwiki%29%2C_black_and_white.png/460px-Periodic_table_%2832-col%2C_enwiki%29%2C_black_and_white.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Periodic_table_%2832-col%2C_enwiki%29%2C_black_and_white.png/690px-Periodic_table_%2832-col%2C_enwiki%29%2C_black_and_white.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Periodic_table_%2832-col%2C_enwiki%29%2C_black_and_white.png/920px-Periodic_table_%2832-col%2C_enwiki%29%2C_black_and_white.png 2x" width="460"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Periodic_table_(32-col,_enwiki),_black_and_white.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The chemical elements ordered in the <a href="/wiki/Periodic_table" title="Periodic table">periodic table</a></div></div></div>
<p>A <b>chemical element</b> is a species of <a href="/wiki/Atom" title="Atom">atoms</a> that have a given number of <a href="/wiki/Proton" title="Proton">protons</a> in their <a href="/wiki/Atomic_nucleus" title="Atomic nucleus">nuclei</a>, including the pure <a href="/wiki/Chemical_substance" title="Chemical substance">substance</a> consisting only of that species.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-goldbookElement_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-goldbookElement-1">[1]</a></sup> Unlike <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chemical_compounds" title="Chemical compounds">chemical compounds</a>, chemical elements cannot be broken down into simpler substances by any chemical reaction. The number of protons in the nucleus is the defining property of an element, and is referred to as its <a href="/wiki/Atomic_number" title="Atomic number">atomic number</a> (represented by the symbol <i>Z</i>) – all atoms with the same atomic number are atoms of the same element.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-goldbookElement_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-goldbookElement-1">[1]</a></sup> Almost all of the <a href="/wiki/Baryon#Baryonic_matter" title="Baryon">baryonic</a> <a href="/wiki/Matter" title="Matter">matter</a> of the universe is composed of chemical elements (among rare exceptions are <a href="/wiki/Neutron_star" title="Neutron star">neutron stars</a>).  When different elements undergo <a href="/wiki/Chemical_reaction" title="Chemical reaction">chemical reactions</a>, atoms are rearranged into new <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chemical_compounds" title="Chemical compounds">compounds</a> held together by <a href="/wiki/Chemical_bond" title="Chemical bond">chemical bonds</a>. Only a minority of elements, such as <a href="/wiki/Silver" title="Silver">silver</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gold" title="Gold">gold</a>, are found uncombined as relatively pure <a href="/wiki/Native_element_mineral" title="Native element mineral">native element minerals</a>. Nearly all other naturally occurring elements occur in the Earth as compounds or <a href="/wiki/Mixture" title="Mixture">mixtures</a>. <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere_of_Earth" title="Atmosphere of Earth">Air</a> is primarily a mixture of the elements <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen" title="Nitrogen">nitrogen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">oxygen</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Argon" title="Argon">argon</a>, though it does contain compounds including <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">carbon dioxide</a> and <a href="/wiki/Water" title="Water">water</a>.
</p><p>The history of the discovery and use of the elements began with <a href="/wiki/Primitive_(phylogenetics)" title="Primitive (phylogenetics)">primitive</a> <a href="/wiki/Society" title="Society">human societies</a> that discovered native minerals like <a href="/wiki/Carbon" title="Carbon">carbon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sulfur" title="Sulfur">sulfur</a>, <a href="/wiki/Copper" title="Copper">copper</a> and gold (though the concept of a chemical element was not yet understood). Attempts to classify materials such as these resulted in the concepts of <a href="/wiki/Classical_element" title="Classical element">classical elements</a>, <a href="/wiki/Alchemy" title="Alchemy">alchemy</a>, and various similar theories throughout human history.  Much of the modern understanding of elements developed from the work of <a href="/wiki/Dmitri_Mendeleev" title="Dmitri Mendeleev">Dmitri Mendeleev</a>, a Russian chemist who published the first recognizable <a href="/wiki/Periodic_table" title="Periodic table">periodic table</a> in 1869. This table organizes the elements by increasing atomic number into rows ("<a href="/wiki/Period_(periodic_table)" title="Period (periodic table)">periods</a>") in which the columns ("<a href="/wiki/Group_(periodic_table)" title="Group (periodic table)">groups</a>") share recurring ("periodic") <a href="/wiki/Physical_property" title="Physical property">physical</a> and <a href="/wiki/Chemical_property" title="Chemical property">chemical properties</a>. The periodic table summarizes various properties of the elements, allowing chemists to derive relationships between them and to make predictions about compounds and potential new ones.
</p><p>By November 2016, the <a href="/wiki/International_Union_of_Pure_and_Applied_Chemistry" title="International Union of Pure and Applied Chemistry">International Union of Pure and Applied Chemistry</a> had recognized a total of 118 elements. The first 94 occur naturally on <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">Earth</a>, and the remaining 24 are <a href="/wiki/Synthetic_element" title="Synthetic element">synthetic elements</a> produced in <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_reaction" title="Nuclear reaction">nuclear reactions</a>. Save for unstable radioactive elements (<a href="/wiki/Radionuclide" title="Radionuclide">radionuclides</a>) which <a href="/wiki/Radioactive_decay" title="Radioactive decay">decay</a> quickly, nearly all of the elements are available industrially in varying amounts.  The <a href="/wiki/Timeline_of_chemical_element_discoveries" title="Timeline of chemical element discoveries">discovery and synthesis of further new elements</a> is an ongoing area of scientific study.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Description">Description</span></h2>
<p>The lightest chemical elements are <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a> and <a href="/wiki/Helium" title="Helium">helium</a>, both created by <a href="/wiki/Big_Bang_nucleosynthesis" title="Big Bang nucleosynthesis">Big Bang nucleosynthesis</a> during the <a href="/wiki/Chronology_of_the_universe" title="Chronology of the universe">first 20 minutes of the universe</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup> in a <a href="/wiki/Ratio" title="Ratio">ratio</a> of around 3:1 by mass (or 12:1 by number of atoms),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> along with tiny traces of the next two elements, <a href="/wiki/Lithium" title="Lithium">lithium</a> and <a href="/wiki/Beryllium" title="Beryllium">beryllium</a>. Almost all other elements found in nature were made by various natural methods of <a href="/wiki/Nucleosynthesis" title="Nucleosynthesis">nucleosynthesis</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup> On <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">Earth</a>, small amounts of new atoms are naturally produced in <a href="/wiki/Nucleogenic" title="Nucleogenic">nucleogenic</a> reactions, or in <a href="/wiki/Environmental_radioactivity#Natural" title="Environmental radioactivity">cosmogenic</a> processes, such as <a href="/wiki/Cosmic_ray_spallation" title="Cosmic ray spallation">cosmic ray spallation</a>. New atoms are also naturally produced on Earth as <a href="/wiki/Radiogenic_nuclide" title="Radiogenic nuclide">radiogenic</a> <a href="/wiki/Decay_product" title="Decay product">daughter isotopes</a> of ongoing <a href="/wiki/Radioactive_decay" title="Radioactive decay">radioactive decay</a> processes such as <a href="/wiki/Alpha_decay" title="Alpha decay">alpha decay</a>, <a href="/wiki/Beta_decay" title="Beta decay">beta decay</a>, <a href="/wiki/Spontaneous_fission" title="Spontaneous fission">spontaneous fission</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cluster_decay" title="Cluster decay">cluster decay</a>, and other rarer modes of decay.
</p><p>Of the 94 naturally occurring elements, those with atomic numbers 1 through 82 each have at least one <a href="/wiki/Stable_nuclide" title="Stable nuclide">stable isotope</a> (except for <a href="/wiki/Technetium" title="Technetium">technetium</a>, element 43 and <a href="/wiki/Promethium" title="Promethium">promethium</a>, element 61, which have no stable isotopes). Isotopes considered stable are those for which no radioactive decay has yet been observed. Elements with atomic numbers 83 through 94 are <a href="/wiki/Radionuclide" title="Radionuclide">unstable</a> to the point that radioactive decay of all isotopes can be detected. Some of these elements, notably <a href="/wiki/Bismuth" title="Bismuth">bismuth</a> (atomic number 83), <a href="/wiki/Thorium" title="Thorium">thorium</a> (atomic number 90), and <a href="/wiki/Uranium" title="Uranium">uranium</a> (atomic number 92), have one or more isotopes with half-lives long enough to survive as remnants of the explosive <a href="/wiki/Stellar_nucleosynthesis" title="Stellar nucleosynthesis">stellar nucleosynthesis</a> that produced the <a href="/wiki/Heavy_metals" title="Heavy metals">heavy metals</a> before the formation of our <a href="/wiki/Solar_System" title="Solar System">Solar System</a>. At over 1.9<span style="margin:0 .15em 0 .25em">×</span>10<sup><span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="7001190000000000000♠"></span>19</span></sup> years, over a billion times longer than the current estimated age of the universe, <a href="/wiki/Bismuth-209" title="Bismuth-209">bismuth-209</a> (atomic number 83) has the longest known alpha decay half-life of any naturally occurring element, and is almost always considered on par with the 80 stable elements.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dume2003_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dume2003-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marcillac2003_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-Marcillac2003-7">[7]</a></sup> The very heaviest elements (those beyond plutonium, element 94) undergo radioactive decay with <a href="/wiki/Half-life" title="Half-life">half-lives</a> so short that they are not found in nature and must be <a href="/wiki/Synthetic_element" title="Synthetic element">synthesized</a>.
</p><p>There are now 118 known elements. In this context, "known" means observed well enough, even from just a few decay products, to have been differentiated from other elements.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schewe_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-Schewe-9">[9]</a></sup> Most recently, the synthesis of element 118 (since named <a href="/wiki/Oganesson" title="Oganesson">oganesson</a>) was reported in October 2006, and the synthesis of element 117 (<a href="/wiki/Tennessine" title="Tennessine">tennessine</a>) was reported in April 2010.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup> Of these 118 elements, 94 occur naturally on Earth. Six of these occur in extreme trace quantities: <a href="/wiki/Technetium" title="Technetium">technetium</a>, atomic number 43; <a href="/wiki/Promethium" title="Promethium">promethium</a>, number 61; <a href="/wiki/Astatine" title="Astatine">astatine</a>, number 85; <a href="/wiki/Francium" title="Francium">francium</a>, number 87; <a href="/wiki/Neptunium" title="Neptunium">neptunium</a>, number 93; and <a href="/wiki/Plutonium" title="Plutonium">plutonium</a>, number 94. These 94 elements have been detected in the universe at large, in the spectra of stars and also supernovae, where short-lived radioactive elements are newly being made. The first 94 elements have been detected directly on Earth as <a href="/wiki/Primordial_nuclide" title="Primordial nuclide">primordial nuclides</a> present from the formation of the <a href="/wiki/Solar_System" title="Solar System">Solar System</a>, or as naturally occurring fission or transmutation products of uranium and thorium.
</p><p>The remaining 24 heavier elements, not found today either on Earth or in astronomical spectra, have been produced artificially: these are all radioactive, with very short half-lives; if any atoms of these elements were present at the formation of Earth, they are extremely likely, to the point of certainty, to have already decayed, and if present in novae have been in quantities too small to have been noted. Technetium was the first purportedly non-naturally occurring element synthesized, in 1937, although trace amounts of technetium have since been found in nature (and also the element may have been discovered naturally in 1925).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> This pattern of artificial production and later natural discovery has been repeated with several other radioactive naturally occurring rare elements.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/List_of_chemical_elements" title="List of chemical elements">List of the elements are available by name, atomic number, density, melting point, boiling point</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Symbol_(chemical_element)" title="Symbol (chemical element)">by symbol</a>, as well as <a href="/wiki/Molar_ionization_energies_of_the_elements" title="Molar ionization energies of the elements">ionization energies of the elements</a>. The nuclides of stable and radioactive elements are also available as a <a href="/wiki/List_of_nuclides" title="List of nuclides">list of nuclides</a>, sorted by length of half-life for those that are unstable. One of the most convenient, and certainly the most traditional presentation of the elements, is in the form of the <a href="/wiki/Periodic_table" title="Periodic table">periodic table</a>, which groups together elements with similar chemical properties (and usually also similar electronic structures).
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Atomic_number">Atomic number</span></h3>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Atomic_number" title="Atomic number">Atomic number</a></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Atomic_number" title="Atomic number">atomic number</a> of an element is equal to the number of protons in each atom, and defines the element.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup> For example, all carbon atoms contain 6 protons in their <a href="/wiki/Atomic_nucleus" title="Atomic nucleus">atomic nucleus</a>; so the atomic number of carbon is 6.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> Carbon atoms may have different numbers of neutrons; atoms of the same element having different numbers of neutrons are known as <a href="/wiki/Isotope" title="Isotope">isotopes</a> of the element.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup>
</p><p>The number of protons in the atomic nucleus also determines its <a href="/wiki/Electric_charge" title="Electric charge">electric charge</a>, which in turn determines the number of <a href="/wiki/Electron" title="Electron">electrons</a> of the atom in its <a href="/wiki/Ionization" title="Ionization">non-ionized</a> state. The electrons are placed into <a href="/wiki/Atomic_orbital" title="Atomic orbital">atomic orbitals</a> that determine the atom's various <a href="/wiki/Chemical_property" title="Chemical property">chemical properties</a>. The number of neutrons in a nucleus usually has very little effect on an element's chemical properties (except in the case of <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a> and <a href="/wiki/Deuterium" title="Deuterium">deuterium</a>). Thus, all carbon isotopes have nearly identical chemical properties because they all have six protons and six electrons, even though carbon atoms may, for example, have 6 or 8 neutrons. That is why the atomic number, rather than <a href="/wiki/Mass_number" title="Mass number">mass number</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Atomic_weight" title="Atomic weight">atomic weight</a>, is considered the identifying characteristic of a chemical element.
</p><p>The symbol for atomic number is <i>Z</i>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Isotopes">Isotopes</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Isotope" title="Isotope">Isotope</a>, <a href="/wiki/Stable_isotope_ratio" title="Stable isotope ratio">Stable isotope ratio</a>, and <a href="/wiki/List_of_nuclides" title="List of nuclides">List of nuclides</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Isotope" title="Isotope">Isotopes</a> are atoms of the same element (that is, with the same number of <a href="/wiki/Proton" title="Proton">protons</a> in their <a href="/wiki/Atomic_nucleus" title="Atomic nucleus">atomic nucleus</a>), but having <i>different</i> numbers of <a href="/wiki/Neutron" title="Neutron">neutrons</a>. Thus, for example, there are three main isotopes of carbon. All carbon atoms have 6 protons in the nucleus, but they can have either 6, 7, or 8 neutrons. Since the mass numbers of these are 12, 13 and 14 respectively, the three isotopes of carbon are known as <a href="/wiki/Carbon-12" title="Carbon-12">carbon-12</a>, <a href="/wiki/Carbon-13" title="Carbon-13">carbon-13</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Carbon-14" title="Carbon-14">carbon-14</a>, often abbreviated to <sup>12</sup>C, <sup>13</sup>C, and <sup>14</sup>C. <a href="/wiki/Carbon" title="Carbon">Carbon</a> in everyday life and in chemistry is a <a href="/wiki/Mixture" title="Mixture">mixture</a> of <sup>12</sup>C (about 98.9%), <sup>13</sup>C (about 1.1%) and about 1 atom per trillion of <sup>14</sup>C.
</p><p>Most (66 of 94) naturally occurring elements have more than one stable isotope. Except for the <a href="/wiki/Isotopes_of_hydrogen" title="Isotopes of hydrogen">isotopes of hydrogen</a> (which differ greatly from each other in relative mass—enough to cause chemical effects), the isotopes of a given element are chemically nearly indistinguishable.
</p><p>All of the elements have some isotopes that are radioactive (<a href="/wiki/Radionuclide" title="Radionuclide">radioisotopes</a>), although not all of these radioisotopes occur naturally. The radioisotopes typically decay into other elements upon radiating an <a href="/wiki/Alpha_particle" title="Alpha particle">alpha</a> or <a href="/wiki/Beta_particle" title="Beta particle">beta particle</a>. If an element has isotopes that are not radioactive, these are termed "stable" isotopes. All of the known stable isotopes occur naturally (see <a href="/wiki/Primordial_nuclide" title="Primordial nuclide">primordial isotope</a>). The many radioisotopes that are not found in nature have been characterized after being artificially made. Certain elements have no stable isotopes and are composed <i>only</i> of radioactive isotopes: specifically the elements without any stable isotopes are technetium (atomic number 43), promethium (atomic number 61), and all observed elements with atomic numbers greater than 82.
</p><p>Of the 80 elements with at least one stable isotope, 26 have only one single stable isotope. The mean number of stable isotopes for the 80 stable elements is 3.1 stable isotopes per element. The largest number of stable isotopes that occur for a single element is 10 (for tin, element 50).
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Isotopic_mass_and_atomic_mass">Isotopic mass and atomic mass</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Atomic_mass" title="Atomic mass">atomic mass</a> and <a href="/wiki/Relative_atomic_mass" title="Relative atomic mass">relative atomic mass</a></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Mass_number" title="Mass number">mass number</a> of an element, <i>A</i>, is the number of <a href="/wiki/Nucleon" title="Nucleon">nucleons</a> (protons and neutrons) in the atomic nucleus. Different isotopes of a given element are distinguished by their mass numbers, which are conventionally written as a superscript on the left hand side of the atomic symbol (e.g. <sup>238</sup>U). The mass number is always a whole number and has units of "nucleons". For example, <a href="/wiki/Isotopes_of_magnesium" title="Isotopes of magnesium">magnesium-24</a> (24 is the mass number) is an atom with 24 nucleons (12 protons and 12 neutrons).
</p><p>Whereas the mass number simply counts the total number of neutrons and protons and is thus a natural (or whole) number, the <a href="/wiki/Atomic_mass" title="Atomic mass">atomic mass</a> of a single atom is a <a href="/wiki/Real_number" title="Real number">real number</a> giving the mass of a particular isotope (or "nuclide") of the element, expressed in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Atomic_mass_unit" title="Atomic mass unit">atomic mass units</a> (symbol: u). In general, the mass number of a given nuclide differs in value slightly from its atomic mass, since the mass of each proton and neutron is not exactly 1 u; since the electrons contribute a lesser share to the atomic mass as neutron number exceeds proton number; and (finally) because of the <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_binding_energy" title="Nuclear binding energy">nuclear binding energy</a>. For example, the atomic mass of chlorine-35 to five significant digits is 34.969 u and that of chlorine-37 is 36.966 u. However, the atomic mass in u of each isotope is quite close to its simple mass number (always within 1%). The only isotope whose atomic mass is exactly a <a href="/wiki/Natural_number" title="Natural number">natural number</a> is <sup>12</sup>C, which by definition has a mass of exactly 12 because u is defined as 1/12 of the mass of a free neutral carbon-12 atom in the ground state.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Standard_atomic_weight" title="Standard atomic weight">standard atomic weight</a> (commonly called "atomic weight") of an element is the <i>average</i> of the atomic masses of all the chemical element's isotopes as found in a particular environment, weighted by isotopic abundance, relative to the atomic mass unit. This number may be a fraction that is <i>not</i> close to a whole number. For example, the relative atomic mass of chlorine is 35.453 u, which differs greatly from a whole number as it is an average of about 76% chlorine-35 and 24% chlorine-37. Whenever a relative atomic mass value differs by more than 1% from a whole number, it is due to this averaging effect, as significant amounts of more than one isotope are naturally present in a sample of that element.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Chemically_pure_and_isotopically_pure">Chemically pure and isotopically pure</span></h3>
<p>Chemists and nuclear scientists have different definitions of a <i>pure element</i>. In chemistry, a pure element means a substance whose atoms all (or in practice almost all) have the same <a href="/wiki/Atomic_number" title="Atomic number">atomic number</a>, or number of <a href="/wiki/Proton" title="Proton">protons</a>. Nuclear scientists, however, define a pure element as one that consists of only one stable <a href="/wiki/Isotope" title="Isotope">isotope</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup>
</p><p>For example, a copper wire is 99.99% chemically pure if 99.99% of its atoms are copper, with 29 protons each. However it is not isotopically pure since ordinary copper consists of two stable isotopes, 69% <sup>63</sup>Cu and 31% <sup>65</sup>Cu, with different numbers of neutrons. However, a pure gold ingot would be both chemically and isotopically pure, since ordinary gold consists only of one isotope, <sup>197</sup>Au.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Allotropes">Allotropes</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Allotropy" title="Allotropy">Allotropy</a></div>
<p>Atoms of chemically pure elements may bond to each other chemically in more than one way, allowing the pure element to exist in multiple <a href="/wiki/Chemical_structure" title="Chemical structure">chemical structures</a> (<a href="/wiki/Molecular_geometry" title="Molecular geometry">spatial arrangements of atoms</a>), known as <a href="/wiki/Allotropy" title="Allotropy">allotropes</a>, which differ in their properties. For example, carbon can be found as <a href="/wiki/Diamond" title="Diamond">diamond</a>, which has a tetrahedral structure around each carbon atom; <a href="/wiki/Graphite" title="Graphite">graphite</a>, which has layers of carbon atoms with a hexagonal structure stacked on top of each other; <a href="/wiki/Graphene" title="Graphene">graphene</a>, which is a single layer of graphite that is very strong; <a href="/wiki/Fullerene" title="Fullerene">fullerenes</a>, which have nearly spherical shapes; and <a href="/wiki/Carbon_nanotube" title="Carbon nanotube">carbon nanotubes</a>, which are tubes with a hexagonal structure (even these may differ from each other in electrical properties). The ability of an element to exist in one of many structural forms is known as 'allotropy'.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Standard_state" title="Standard state">standard state</a>, also known as the reference state, of an element is defined as its thermodynamically most stable state at a pressure of 1 <a href="/wiki/Bar_(unit)" title="Bar (unit)">bar</a> and a given temperature (typically at 298.15K). In <a href="/wiki/Thermochemistry" title="Thermochemistry">thermochemistry</a>, an element is defined to have an <a href="/wiki/Standard_enthalpy_of_formation" title="Standard enthalpy of formation">enthalpy of formation</a> of zero in its standard state. For example, the reference state for carbon is graphite, because the structure of graphite is more stable than that of the other allotropes.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Properties">Properties</span></h3>
<p>Several kinds of descriptive categorizations can be applied broadly to the elements, including consideration of their general physical and chemical properties, their states of matter under familiar conditions, their melting and boiling points, their densities, their crystal structures as solids, and their origins.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="General_properties">General properties</span></h4>
<p>Several terms are commonly used to characterize the general physical and chemical properties of the chemical elements. A first distinction is between <a href="/wiki/Metal" title="Metal">metals</a>, which readily conduct <a href="/wiki/Electricity" title="Electricity">electricity</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nonmetal" title="Nonmetal">nonmetals</a>, which do not, and a small group, (the <i><a href="/wiki/Metalloid" title="Metalloid">metalloids</a></i>), having intermediate properties and often behaving as <a href="/wiki/Semiconductor" title="Semiconductor">semiconductors</a>.
</p><p>A more refined classification is often shown in colored presentations of the periodic table. This system restricts the terms "metal" and "nonmetal" to only certain of the more broadly defined metals and nonmetals, adding additional terms for certain sets of the more broadly viewed metals and nonmetals. The version of this classification used in the periodic tables presented here includes: <a href="/wiki/Actinide" title="Actinide">actinides</a>, <a href="/wiki/Alkali_metal" title="Alkali metal">alkali metals</a>, <a href="/wiki/Alkaline_earth_metal" title="Alkaline earth metal">alkaline earth metals</a>, <a href="/wiki/Halogen" title="Halogen">halogens</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lanthanide" title="Lanthanide">lanthanides</a>, <a href="/wiki/Transition_metal" title="Transition metal">transition metals</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Post-transition_metal" title="Post-transition metal">post-transition metals</a>, <a href="/wiki/Metalloid" title="Metalloid">metalloids</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Reactive_nonmetal" title="Reactive nonmetal">reactive nonmetals</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Noble_gas" title="Noble gas">noble gases</a>. In this system, the alkali metals, alkaline earth metals, and transition metals, as well as the lanthanides and the actinides, are special groups of the metals viewed in a broader sense. Similarly, the reactive nonmetals and the noble gases are nonmetals viewed in the broader sense. In some presentations, the halogens are not distinguished, with <a href="/wiki/Astatine" title="Astatine">astatine</a> identified as a metalloid and the others identified as nonmetals.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="States_of_matter">States of matter</span></h4>
<p>Another commonly used basic distinction among the elements is their <a href="/wiki/State_of_matter" title="State of matter">state of matter</a> (phase), whether <a href="/wiki/Solid" title="Solid">solid</a>, <a href="/wiki/Liquid" title="Liquid">liquid</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Gas" title="Gas">gas</a>, at a selected <a href="/wiki/Standard_temperature_and_pressure" title="Standard temperature and pressure">standard temperature and pressure</a> (STP). Most of the elements are solids at conventional temperatures and atmospheric pressure, while several are gases. Only <a href="/wiki/Bromine" title="Bromine">bromine</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mercury_(element)" title="Mercury (element)">mercury</a> are liquids at 0 degrees Celsius (32 degrees Fahrenheit) and normal atmospheric pressure; <a href="/wiki/Caesium" title="Caesium">caesium</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gallium" title="Gallium">gallium</a> are solids at that temperature, but melt at 28.4 °C (83.2 °F) and 29.8 °C (85.6 °F), respectively.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Melting_and_boiling_points">Melting and boiling points</span></h4>
<p><a href="/wiki/Melting_point" title="Melting point">Melting</a> and <a href="/wiki/Boiling_point" title="Boiling point">boiling points</a>, typically expressed in <a href="/wiki/Celsius" title="Celsius">degrees Celsius</a> at a pressure of one atmosphere, are commonly used in characterizing the various elements. While known for most elements, either or both of these measurements is still undetermined for some of the radioactive elements available in only tiny quantities. Since <a href="/wiki/Helium" title="Helium">helium</a> remains a liquid even at <a href="/wiki/Absolute_zero" title="Absolute zero">absolute zero</a> at atmospheric pressure, it has only a boiling point, and not a melting point, in conventional presentations.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Densities">Densities</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Densities_of_the_elements_(data_page)" title="Densities of the elements (data page)">Densities of the elements (data page)</a></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Density" title="Density">density</a> at selected standard temperature and pressure (<a href="/wiki/Standard_temperature_and_pressure" title="Standard temperature and pressure">STP</a>) is frequently used in characterizing the elements. Density is often expressed in <a href="/wiki/Gram" title="Gram">grams</a> per cubic centimeter (g/cm<sup>3</sup>). Since several elements are gases at commonly encountered temperatures, their densities are usually stated for their gaseous forms; when liquefied or solidified, the gaseous elements have densities similar to those of the other elements.
</p><p>When an element has <a href="/wiki/Allotropy" title="Allotropy">allotropes</a> with different densities, one representative allotrope is typically selected in summary presentations, while densities for each allotrope can be stated where more detail is provided. For example, the three familiar <a href="/wiki/Allotropes_of_carbon" title="Allotropes of carbon">allotropes of carbon</a> (<a href="/wiki/Amorphous_carbon" title="Amorphous carbon">amorphous carbon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Graphite" title="Graphite">graphite</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Diamond" title="Diamond">diamond</a>) have densities of 1.8–2.1, 2.267, and 3.515 g/cm<sup>3</sup>, respectively.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Crystal_structures">Crystal structures</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Crystal_structure" title="Crystal structure">Crystal structure</a></div>
<p>The elements studied to date as solid samples have eight kinds of <a href="/wiki/Crystal_structure" title="Crystal structure">crystal structures</a>: <a href="/wiki/Cubic_crystal_system" title="Cubic crystal system">cubic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cubic_crystal_system" title="Cubic crystal system">body-centered cubic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cubic_crystal_system" title="Cubic crystal system">face-centered cubic</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hexagonal_crystal_system" title="Hexagonal crystal system">hexagonal</a>, <a href="/wiki/Monoclinic_crystal_system" title="Monoclinic crystal system">monoclinic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Orthorhombic_crystal_system" title="Orthorhombic crystal system">orthorhombic</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Trigonal_crystal_system" title="Trigonal crystal system">rhombohedral</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Tetragonal_crystal_system" title="Tetragonal crystal system">tetragonal</a>. For some of the synthetically produced transuranic elements, available samples have been too small to determine crystal structures.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Occurrence_and_origin_on_Earth">Occurrence and origin on Earth</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Abundance_of_elements_in_Earth%27s_crust" title="Abundance of elements in Earth's crust">Abundance of elements in Earth's crust</a></div>
<p>Chemical elements may also be categorized by their origin on Earth, with the first 94 considered naturally occurring, while those with atomic numbers beyond 94 have only been produced artificially as the synthetic products of man-made nuclear reactions.
</p><p>Of the 94 naturally occurring elements, 83 are considered <a href="/wiki/Primordial_nuclide" title="Primordial nuclide">primordial</a> and either <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stable_isotope" title="Stable isotope">stable</a> or weakly radioactive. The remaining 11 naturally occurring elements possess <a href="/wiki/Half-life" title="Half-life">half lives</a> too short for them to have been present at the beginning of the <a href="/wiki/Solar_System" title="Solar System">Solar System</a>, and are therefore considered transient elements. Of these 11 transient elements, 5 (<a href="/wiki/Polonium" title="Polonium">polonium</a>, <a href="/wiki/Radon" title="Radon">radon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Radium" title="Radium">radium</a>, <a href="/wiki/Actinium" title="Actinium">actinium</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Protactinium" title="Protactinium">protactinium</a>) are relatively common <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Decay_products" title="Decay products">decay products</a> of <a href="/wiki/Thorium" title="Thorium">thorium</a> and <a href="/wiki/Uranium" title="Uranium">uranium</a>. The remaining 6 transient elements (<a href="/wiki/Technetium" title="Technetium">technetium</a>, <a href="/wiki/Promethium" title="Promethium">promethium</a>, <a href="/wiki/Astatine" title="Astatine">astatine</a>, <a href="/wiki/Francium" title="Francium">francium</a>, <a href="/wiki/Neptunium" title="Neptunium">neptunium</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Plutonium" title="Plutonium">plutonium</a>) occur only rarely, as products of rare decay modes or nuclear reaction processes involving uranium or other heavy elements.
</p><p>No radioactive decay has been observed for elements with atomic numbers 1 through 82, except 43 (<a href="/wiki/Technetium" title="Technetium">technetium</a>) and 61 (<a href="/wiki/Promethium" title="Promethium">promethium</a>). Observationally stable isotopes of some elements (such as <a href="/wiki/Tungsten" title="Tungsten">tungsten</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lead" title="Lead">lead</a>), however, are predicted to be slightly radioactive with very long half-lives:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NUBASE2016_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-NUBASE2016-18">[18]</a></sup> for example, the half-lives predicted for the observationally stable lead isotopes range from 10<sup>35</sup> to 10<sup>189</sup> years. Elements with atomic numbers 43, 61, and 83 through 94 are unstable enough that their radioactive decay can readily be detected. Three of these elements, bismuth (element 83), thorium (element 90), and uranium (element 92) have one or more isotopes with half-lives long enough to survive as remnants of the explosive <a href="/wiki/Stellar_nucleosynthesis" title="Stellar nucleosynthesis">stellar nucleosynthesis</a> that produced the heavy elements before the formation of the <a href="/wiki/Solar_System" title="Solar System">Solar System</a>. For example, at over 1.9<span style="margin:0 .15em 0 .25em">×</span>10<sup><span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="7001190000000000000♠"></span>19</span></sup> years, over a billion times longer than the current estimated age of the universe, <a href="/wiki/Bismuth-209" title="Bismuth-209">bismuth-209</a> has the longest known <a href="/wiki/Alpha_decay" title="Alpha decay">alpha decay</a> half-life of any naturally occurring element.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dume2003_6-1"><a href="#cite_note-Dume2003-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marcillac2003_7-1"><a href="#cite_note-Marcillac2003-7">[7]</a></sup> The very heaviest 24 elements (those beyond plutonium, element 94) undergo radioactive decay with short half-lives and cannot be produced as daughters of longer-lived elements, and thus are not known to occur in nature at all.
</p>

</div>
</div></div>
<p>The properties of the chemical elements are often summarized using the <a href="/wiki/Periodic_table" title="Periodic table">periodic table</a>, which powerfully and elegantly organizes the elements by increasing atomic number into rows (<a href="/wiki/Period_(periodic_table)" title="Period (periodic table)">"periods"</a>) in which the columns (<a href="/wiki/Group_(periodic_table)" title="Group (periodic table)">"groups"</a>) share recurring ("periodic") physical and chemical properties. The current standard table contains 118 confirmed elements as of 2021.
</p><p>Although earlier precursors to this presentation exist, its invention is generally credited to the Russian chemist <a href="/wiki/Dmitri_Mendeleev" title="Dmitri Mendeleev">Dmitri Mendeleev</a> in 1869, who intended the table to illustrate recurring trends in the properties of the elements. The layout of the table has been refined and extended over time as new elements have been discovered and new theoretical models have been developed to explain chemical behavior.
</p><p>Use of the periodic table is now ubiquitous within the academic discipline of chemistry, providing an extremely useful framework to classify, systematize and compare all the many different forms of chemical behavior. The table has also found wide application in <a href="/wiki/Physics" title="Physics">physics</a>, <a href="/wiki/Geology" title="Geology">geology</a>, <a href="/wiki/Biology" title="Biology">biology</a>, <a href="/wiki/Materials_science" title="Materials science">materials science</a>, <a href="/wiki/Engineering" title="Engineering">engineering</a>, <a href="/wiki/Agriculture" title="Agriculture">agriculture</a>, <a href="/wiki/Medicine" title="Medicine">medicine</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nutrition" title="Nutrition">nutrition</a>, <a href="/wiki/Environmental_health" title="Environmental health">environmental health</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Astronomy" title="Astronomy">astronomy</a>. Its principles are especially important in <a href="/wiki/Chemical_engineering" title="Chemical engineering">chemical engineering</a>.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Nomenclature_and_symbols">Nomenclature and symbols</span></h2>
<p><span class="anchor" id="Nomenclature"></span>
</p><p>The various chemical elements are formally identified by their unique <a href="/wiki/Atomic_number" title="Atomic number">atomic numbers</a>, by their accepted names, and by their <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Symbol_(chemistry)" title="Symbol (chemistry)">symbols</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Atomic_numbers">Atomic numbers</span></h3>
<p>The known elements have atomic numbers from 1 through 118, conventionally presented as <a href="/wiki/Arabic_numerals" title="Arabic numerals">Arabic numerals</a>. Since the elements can be uniquely sequenced by atomic number, conventionally from lowest to highest (as in a <a href="/wiki/Periodic_table" title="Periodic table">periodic table</a>), sets of elements are sometimes specified by such notation as "through", "beyond", or "from ... through", as in "through iron", "beyond uranium", or "from lanthanum through lutetium". The terms "light" and "heavy" are sometimes also used informally to indicate relative atomic numbers (not densities), as in "lighter than carbon" or "heavier than lead", although technically the weight or mass of atoms of an element (their atomic weights or atomic masses) do not always increase <a href="/wiki/Monotonic_function" title="Monotonic function">monotonically</a> with their atomic numbers.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Element_names">Element names</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Naming_of_elements" title="Naming of elements">Naming of elements</a></div>
<p>The naming of various substances now known as elements precedes the <a href="/wiki/Atomic_theory" title="Atomic theory">atomic theory of matter</a>, as names were given locally by various cultures to various minerals, metals, compounds, alloys, mixtures, and other materials, although at the time it was not known which chemicals were elements and which compounds. As they were identified as elements, the existing names for anciently known elements (e.g., gold, mercury, iron) were kept in most countries. National differences emerged over the names of elements either for convenience, linguistic niceties, or nationalism. For a few illustrative examples: German speakers use "Wasserstoff" (water substance) for "hydrogen", "Sauerstoff" (acid substance) for "oxygen" and "Stickstoff" (smothering substance) for "nitrogen", while English and some <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Romance_language" title="Romance language">romance languages</a> use "sodium" for "natrium" and "potassium" for "kalium", and the French, Italians, Greeks, Portuguese and Poles prefer "azote/azot/azoto" (from roots meaning "no life") for "nitrogen".
</p><p>For purposes of international communication and trade, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Table_of_chemical_elements" title="Table of chemical elements">official names of the chemical elements</a> both ancient and more recently recognized are decided by the <a href="/wiki/International_Union_of_Pure_and_Applied_Chemistry" title="International Union of Pure and Applied Chemistry">International Union of Pure and Applied Chemistry</a> (IUPAC), which has decided on a sort of international English language, drawing on traditional English names even when an element's chemical symbol is based on a Latin or other traditional word, for example adopting "gold" rather than "aurum" as the name for the 79th element (Au). IUPAC prefers the British spellings "<a href="/wiki/Aluminium" title="Aluminium">aluminium</a>" and "caesium" over the U.S. spellings "aluminum" and "cesium", and the U.S. "sulfur" over the British "sulphur". However, elements that are practical to sell in bulk in many countries often still have locally used national names, and countries whose national language does not use the <a href="/wiki/Latin_alphabet" title="Latin alphabet">Latin alphabet</a> are likely to use the IUPAC element names.
</p><p>According to IUPAC, chemical elements are not proper nouns in English; consequently, the full name of an element is not routinely capitalized in English, even if derived from a <a href="/wiki/Proper_noun" title="Proper noun">proper noun</a>, as in <a href="/wiki/Californium" title="Californium">californium</a> and <a href="/wiki/Einsteinium" title="Einsteinium">einsteinium</a>. Isotope names of chemical elements are also uncapitalized if written out, <i>e.g.,</i> <a href="/wiki/Carbon-12" title="Carbon-12">carbon-12</a> or <a href="/wiki/Uranium-235" title="Uranium-235">uranium-235</a>. Chemical element <i>symbols</i> (such as Cf for californium and Es for einsteinium), are always capitalized (see below).
</p><p>In the second half of the twentieth century, physics laboratories became able to produce nuclei of chemical elements with half-lives too short for an appreciable amount of them to exist at any time. These are also named by IUPAC, which generally adopts the name chosen by the discoverer. This practice can lead to the controversial question of which research group actually discovered an element, a question that delayed the naming of elements with atomic number of 104 and higher for a considerable amount of time. (See <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Element_naming_controversy" title="Element naming controversy">element naming controversy</a>).
</p><p>Precursors of such controversies involved the nationalistic namings of elements in the late 19th century. For example, <i><a href="/wiki/Lutetium" title="Lutetium">lutetium</a></i> was named in reference to Paris, France. The Germans were reluctant to relinquish naming rights to the French, often calling it <i>cassiopeium</i>. Similarly, the British discoverer of <i><a href="/wiki/Niobium" title="Niobium">niobium</a></i> originally named it <i>columbium,</i> in reference to the <a href="/wiki/New_World" title="New World">New World</a>. It was used extensively as such by American publications before the international standardization (in 1950).
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Chemical_symbols">Chemical symbols</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">For listings of current chemical symbols, symbols not currently used, and other symbols that may look like chemical symbols, see <a href="/wiki/Chemical_symbol" title="Chemical symbol">Chemical symbol</a>.</div>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Specific_chemical_elements">Specific chemical elements</span></h4>
<p>Before chemistry became a <a href="/wiki/Science" title="Science">science</a>, <a href="/wiki/Alchemy" title="Alchemy">alchemists</a> had designed arcane symbols for both metals and common compounds. These were however used as abbreviations in diagrams or procedures; there was no concept of atoms combining to form <a href="/wiki/Molecule" title="Molecule">molecules</a>. With his advances in the atomic theory of matter, <a href="/wiki/John_Dalton" title="John Dalton">John Dalton</a> devised his own simpler symbols, based on circles, to depict molecules.
</p><p>The current system of chemical notation was invented by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/J%C3%B6ns_Jakob_Berzelius" title="Jöns Jakob Berzelius">Berzelius</a>. In this typographical system, chemical symbols are not mere abbreviations—though each consists of letters of the <a href="/wiki/Latin_alphabet" title="Latin alphabet">Latin alphabet</a>. They are intended as universal symbols for people of all languages and alphabets.
</p><p>The first of these symbols were intended to be fully universal. Since Latin was the common language of science at that time, they were abbreviations based on the <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a> names of metals. Cu comes from cuprum, Fe comes from ferrum, Ag from argentum. The symbols were not followed by a period (full stop) as with abbreviations. Later chemical elements were also assigned unique chemical symbols, based on the name of the element, but not necessarily in English. For example, <a href="/wiki/Sodium" title="Sodium">sodium</a> has the chemical symbol 'Na' after the Latin <i>natrium</i>. The same applies to "Fe" (ferrum) for <a href="/wiki/Iron" title="Iron">iron</a>, "Hg" (hydrargyrum) for <a href="/wiki/Mercury_(element)" title="Mercury (element)">mercury</a>, "Sn" (stannum) for <a href="/wiki/Tin" title="Tin">tin</a>, "Au" (aurum) for <a href="/wiki/Gold" title="Gold">gold</a>, "Ag" (argentum) for <a href="/wiki/Silver" title="Silver">silver</a>, "Pb" (plumbum) for <a href="/wiki/Lead" title="Lead">lead</a>, "Cu" (cuprum) for <a href="/wiki/Copper" title="Copper">copper</a>, and "Sb" (stibium) for <a href="/wiki/Antimony" title="Antimony">antimony</a>. "W" (wolfram) for <a href="/wiki/Tungsten" title="Tungsten">tungsten</a> ultimately derives from German, "K" (kalium) for <a href="/wiki/Potassium" title="Potassium">potassium</a> ultimately from Arabic.
</p><p>Chemical symbols are understood internationally when element names might require translation. There have sometimes been differences in the past. For example, Germans in the past have used "J" (for the alternate name Jod) for iodine, but now use "I" and "Iod".
</p><p>The first letter of a chemical symbol is always capitalized, as in the preceding examples, and the subsequent letters, if any, are always lower case (small letters). Thus, the symbols for californium and einsteinium are Cf and Es.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="General_chemical_symbols">General chemical symbols</span></h4>
<p>There are also symbols in chemical equations for groups of chemical elements, for example in comparative formulas. These are often a single capital letter, and the letters are reserved and not used for names of specific elements. For example, an "<b>X</b>" indicates a variable group (usually a <a href="/wiki/Halogen" title="Halogen">halogen</a>) in a class of compounds, while "<b>R</b>" is a <a href="/wiki/Radical_(chemistry)" title="Radical (chemistry)">radical</a>, meaning a compound structure such as a hydrocarbon chain. The letter "<b>Q</b>" is reserved for "heat" in a chemical reaction. "<b>Y</b>" is also often used as a general chemical symbol, although it is also the symbol of <a href="/wiki/Yttrium" title="Yttrium">yttrium</a>. "<b>Z</b>" is also frequently used as a general variable group. "<b>E</b>" is used in organic chemistry to denote an <a href="/wiki/Polar_effect" title="Polar effect">electron-withdrawing group</a> or an <a href="/wiki/Electrophile" title="Electrophile">electrophile</a>; similarly "<b>Nu</b>" denotes a <a href="/wiki/Nucleophile" title="Nucleophile">nucleophile</a>. "<b>L</b>" is used to represent a general <a href="/wiki/Ligand" title="Ligand">ligand</a> in <a href="/wiki/Inorganic_chemistry" title="Inorganic chemistry">inorganic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Organometallic_chemistry" title="Organometallic chemistry">organometallic chemistry</a>. "<b>M</b>" is also often used in place of a general metal.
</p><p>At least two additional, two-letter generic chemical symbols are also in informal usage, "<b>Ln</b>" for any <a href="/wiki/Lanthanide" title="Lanthanide">lanthanide</a> element and "<b>An</b>" for any <a href="/wiki/Actinide" title="Actinide">actinide</a> element. "<b>Rg</b>" was formerly used for any <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rare_gas" title="Rare gas">rare gas</a> element, but the group of rare gases has now been renamed <a href="/wiki/Noble_gas" title="Noble gas">noble gases</a> and the symbol "<b>Rg</b>" has now been assigned to the element <a href="/wiki/Roentgenium" title="Roentgenium">roentgenium</a>.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Isotope_symbols">Isotope symbols</span></h4>
<p>Isotopes are distinguished by the atomic mass number (total protons and neutrons) for a particular isotope of an element, with this number combined with the pertinent element's symbol. IUPAC prefers that isotope symbols be written in superscript notation when practical, for example <sup>12</sup>C and <sup>235</sup>U. However, other notations, such as carbon-12 and uranium-235, or C-12 and U-235, are also used.
</p><p>As a special case, the three naturally occurring isotopes of the element <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a> are often specified as <b>H</b> for <sup>1</sup>H (<a href="/wiki/Isotopes_of_hydrogen#Hydrogen-1_(Protium)" title="Isotopes of hydrogen">protium</a>), <b>D</b> for <sup>2</sup>H (<a href="/wiki/Deuterium" title="Deuterium">deuterium</a>), and <b>T</b> for <sup>3</sup>H (<a href="/wiki/Tritium" title="Tritium">tritium</a>). This convention is easier to use in chemical equations, replacing the need to write out the mass number for each atom. For example, the formula for <a href="/wiki/Heavy_water" title="Heavy water">heavy water</a> may be written D<sub>2</sub>O instead of <sup>2</sup>H<sub>2</sub>O.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Origin_of_the_elements">Origin of the elements</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:372px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Universe_content_pie_chart.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1200" data-file-width="850" decoding="async" height="522" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Universe_content_pie_chart.jpg/370px-Universe_content_pie_chart.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Universe_content_pie_chart.jpg/555px-Universe_content_pie_chart.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Universe_content_pie_chart.jpg/740px-Universe_content_pie_chart.jpg 2x" width="370"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Universe_content_pie_chart.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Estimated distribution of dark matter and dark energy in the universe. Only the fraction of the mass and energy in the universe labeled "atoms" is composed of chemical elements.</div></div></div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1097763485">.mw-parser-output .ambox{border:1px solid #a2a9b1;border-left:10px solid #36c;background-color:#fbfbfb;box-sizing:border-box}.mw-parser-output .mbox-text-span{margin-left:23px!important}@media(min-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .ambox{margin:0 10%}}</style><table class="box-More_citations_needed plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Refimprove" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div class="mbox-image-div"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Question_book-new.svg"><img alt="" data-file-height="399" <b>needs additional citations for <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">verification</a></b>.<span class="hide-when-compact"> Please help <a class="external text" beginners">adding citations to reliable sources</a>. Unsourced material may be challenged and removed.<br/><small><span class="plainlinks"><i>Find sources:</i> <a class="external text" href="//www.google.com/search?as_eq=wikipedia&amp;q=%22Chemical+element%22" rel="nofollow">"Chemical element"</a> – <a class="external text" href="//www.google.com/search?tbm=nws&amp;q=%22Chemical+element%22+-wikipedia&amp;tbs=ar:1" rel="nofollow">news</a> <b>·</b> <a class="external text" href="//www.google.com/search?&amp;q=%22Chemical+element%22&amp;tbs=bkt:s&amp;tbm=bks" rel="nofollow">newspapers</a> <b>·</b> <a class="external text" href="//www.google.com/search?tbs=bks:1&amp;q=%22Chemical+element%22+-wikipedia" rel="nofollow">books</a> <b>·</b> <a class="external text" href="//scholar.google.com/scholar?q=%22Chemical+element%22" rel="nofollow">scholar</a> <b>·</b> <a class="external text" href="https://www.jstor.org/action/doBasicSearch?Query=%22Chemical+element%22&amp;acc=on&amp;wc=on" rel="nofollow">JSTOR</a></span></small></span> <span class="date-container"><i>(<span class="date">April 2021</span>)</i></span><span class="hide-when-compact"><i> (<small><a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a></small>)</i></span></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Nucleosynthesis" title="Nucleosynthesis">Nucleosynthesis</a></div>
<p>Only about 4% of the total mass of the universe is made of atoms or <a href="/wiki/Ion" title="Ion">ions</a>, and thus represented by chemical elements. This fraction is about 15% of the total matter, with the remainder of the matter (85%) being <a href="/wiki/Dark_matter" title="Dark matter">dark matter</a>. The nature of dark matter is unknown, but it is not composed of atoms of chemical elements because it contains no protons, neutrons, or electrons. (The remaining non-matter part of the mass of the universe is composed of the even less well understood <a href="/wiki/Dark_energy" title="Dark energy">dark energy</a>).
</p><p>The 94 naturally occurring chemical elements were produced by at least four classes of astrophysical process. Most of the <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Helium" title="Helium">helium</a> and a very small quantity of <a href="/wiki/Lithium" title="Lithium">lithium</a> were produced in the first few minutes of the <a href="/wiki/Big_Bang" title="Big Bang">Big Bang</a>. This <a href="/wiki/Big_Bang_nucleosynthesis" title="Big Bang nucleosynthesis">Big Bang nucleosynthesis</a> happened only once; the other processes are ongoing. <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_fusion" title="Nuclear fusion">Nuclear fusion</a> inside stars produces elements through <a href="/wiki/Stellar_nucleosynthesis" title="Stellar nucleosynthesis">stellar nucleosynthesis</a>, including all elements from <a href="/wiki/Carbon" title="Carbon">carbon</a> to <a href="/wiki/Iron" title="Iron">iron</a> in atomic number. Elements higher in atomic number than iron, including heavy elements like <a href="/wiki/Uranium" title="Uranium">uranium</a> and <a href="/wiki/Plutonium" title="Plutonium">plutonium</a>, are produced by various forms of explosive nucleosynthesis in <a href="/wiki/Supernova" title="Supernova">supernovae</a> and <a href="/wiki/Neutron_star_merger" title="Neutron star merger">neutron star mergers</a>. The light elements <a href="/wiki/Lithium" title="Lithium">lithium</a>, <a href="/wiki/Beryllium" title="Beryllium">beryllium</a> and <a href="/wiki/Boron" title="Boron">boron</a> are produced mostly through <a href="/wiki/Cosmic_ray_spallation" title="Cosmic ray spallation">cosmic ray spallation</a> (fragmentation induced by <a href="/wiki/Cosmic_ray" title="Cosmic ray">cosmic rays</a>) of carbon, <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen" title="Nitrogen">nitrogen</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">oxygen</a>.
</p><p>During the early phases of the Big Bang, <a href="/wiki/Big_Bang_nucleosynthesis" title="Big Bang nucleosynthesis">nucleosynthesis</a> of hydrogen nuclei resulted in the production of hydrogen-1 (<a href="/wiki/Isotopes_of_hydrogen#Hydrogen-1_(Protium)" title="Isotopes of hydrogen">protium</a>, <sup>1</sup>H) and helium-4 (<sup>4</sup>He), as well as a smaller amount of <a href="/wiki/Deuterium" title="Deuterium">deuterium</a> (<sup>2</sup>H) and very minuscule amounts (on the order of 10<sup>−10</sup>) of lithium and beryllium. Even smaller amounts of boron may have been produced in the Big Bang, since it has been observed in some very old stars, while carbon has not.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> No elements heavier than boron were produced in the Big Bang. As a result, the primordial abundance of atoms (or ions) consisted of roughly 75% <sup>1</sup>H, 25% <sup>4</sup>He, and 0.01% deuterium, with only tiny traces of lithium, beryllium, and perhaps boron.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> Subsequent enrichment of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Galactic_spheroid" title="Galactic spheroid">galactic halos</a> occurred due to stellar nucleosynthesis and <a href="/wiki/Supernova_nucleosynthesis" title="Supernova nucleosynthesis">supernova nucleosynthesis</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-synthesis_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-synthesis-23">[23]</a></sup> However, the element abundance in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Intergalactic_space" title="Intergalactic space">intergalactic space</a> can still closely resemble primordial conditions, unless it has been enriched by some means.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:502px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Nucleosynthesis_periodic_table.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="256" data-file-width="512" decoding="async" height="250" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/Nucleosynthesis_periodic_table.svg/500px-Nucleosynthesis_periodic_table.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/Nucleosynthesis_periodic_table.svg/750px-Nucleosynthesis_periodic_table.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/Nucleosynthesis_periodic_table.svg/1000px-Nucleosynthesis_periodic_table.svg.png 2x" width="500"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Nucleosynthesis_periodic_table.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Periodic table showing the cosmogenic origin of each element in the Big Bang, or in large or small stars. Small stars can produce certain elements up to sulfur, by the <a href="/wiki/Alpha_process" title="Alpha process">alpha process</a>. Supernovae are needed to produce "heavy" elements (those beyond iron and nickel) rapidly by neutron buildup, in the <a href="/wiki/R-process" title="R-process">r-process</a>. Certain large stars slowly produce other elements heavier than iron, in the <a href="/wiki/S-process" title="S-process">s-process</a>; these may then be blown into space in the off-gassing of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Planetary_nebulae" title="Planetary nebulae">planetary nebulae</a></div></div></div>
<p>On Earth (and elsewhere), trace amounts of various elements continue to be produced from other elements as products of <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_transmutation" title="Nuclear transmutation">nuclear transmutation</a> processes. These include some produced by <a href="/wiki/Cosmic_ray" title="Cosmic ray">cosmic rays</a> or other nuclear reactions (see <a href="/wiki/Cosmogenic_nuclide" title="Cosmogenic nuclide">cosmogenic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nucleogenic" title="Nucleogenic">nucleogenic</a> nuclides), and others produced as <a href="/wiki/Decay_product" title="Decay product">decay products</a> of long-lived <a href="/wiki/Primordial_nuclide" title="Primordial nuclide">primordial nuclides</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Earnshaw1997_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-Earnshaw1997-24">[24]</a></sup> For example, trace (but detectable) amounts of <a href="/wiki/Carbon-14" title="Carbon-14">carbon-14</a> (<sup>14</sup>C) are continually produced in the atmosphere by cosmic rays impacting <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen" title="Nitrogen">nitrogen</a> atoms, and argon-40 (<sup>40</sup>Ar) is continually produced by the decay of primordially occurring but unstable potassium-40 (<sup>40</sup>K). Also, three primordially occurring but radioactive <a href="/wiki/Actinide" title="Actinide">actinides</a>, <a href="/wiki/Thorium" title="Thorium">thorium</a>, uranium, and plutonium, decay through a series of recurrently produced but unstable radioactive elements such as radium and <a href="/wiki/Radon" title="Radon">radon</a>, which are transiently present in any sample of these metals or their ores or compounds. Three other radioactive elements, <a href="/wiki/Technetium" title="Technetium">technetium</a>, <a href="/wiki/Promethium" title="Promethium">promethium</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Neptunium" title="Neptunium">neptunium</a>, occur only incidentally in natural materials, produced as individual atoms by <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_fission" title="Nuclear fission">nuclear fission</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Atomic_nucleus" title="Atomic nucleus">nuclei</a> of various heavy elements or in other rare nuclear processes.
</p><p>In addition to the 94 naturally occurring elements, several <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Artificial_element" title="Artificial element">artificial elements</a> have been produced by human <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_physics" title="Nuclear physics">nuclear physics</a> technology. As of 2021<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Chemical_element&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, these experiments have produced all elements up to atomic number 118.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Abundance">Abundance</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Abundance_of_the_chemical_elements" title="Abundance of the chemical elements">Abundance of the chemical elements</a></div>
<p>The following graph (note log scale) shows the abundance of elements in our <a href="/wiki/Solar_System" title="Solar System">Solar System</a>. The table shows the twelve most common elements in our galaxy (estimated spectroscopically), as measured in <a href="/wiki/Parts-per_notation" title="Parts-per notation">parts per million</a>, by <a href="/wiki/Mass" title="Mass">mass</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-croswell_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-croswell-25">[25]</a></sup> Nearby galaxies that have evolved along similar lines have a corresponding enrichment of elements heavier than hydrogen and helium. The more distant galaxies are being viewed as they appeared in the past, so their abundances of elements appear closer to the primordial mixture. As physical laws and processes appear common throughout the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Visible_universe" title="Visible universe">visible universe</a>, however, scientist expect that these galaxies evolved elements in similar abundance.
</p><p>The abundance of elements in the Solar System is in keeping with their origin from nucleosynthesis in the <a href="/wiki/Big_Bang" title="Big Bang">Big Bang</a> and a number of progenitor supernova stars. Very abundant hydrogen and helium are products of the Big Bang, but the next three elements are rare since they had little time to form in the Big Bang and are not made in stars (they are, however, produced in small quantities by the breakup of heavier elements in interstellar dust, as a result of impact by <a href="/wiki/Cosmic_ray" title="Cosmic ray">cosmic rays</a>). Beginning with carbon, elements are produced in stars by buildup from alpha particles (helium nuclei), resulting in an alternatingly larger abundance of elements with even atomic numbers (these are also more stable). In general, such elements up to iron are made in large stars in the process of becoming <a href="/wiki/Supernova" title="Supernova">supernovas</a>. Iron-56 is particularly common, since it is the most stable element that can easily be made from alpha particles (being a product of decay of radioactive nickel-56, ultimately made from 14 helium nuclei). Elements heavier than iron are made in energy-absorbing processes in large stars, and their abundance in the universe (and on Earth) generally decreases with their atomic number.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Abundance_of_the_chemical_elements" title="Abundance of the chemical elements">abundance of the chemical elements</a> on Earth varies from air to crust to ocean, and in various types of life. The abundance of elements in Earth's crust differs from that in the Solar System (as seen in the Sun and heavy planets like Jupiter) mainly in selective loss of the very lightest elements (hydrogen and helium) and also volatile neon, carbon (as hydrocarbons), nitrogen and sulfur, as a result of solar heating in the early formation of the solar system. Oxygen, the most abundant Earth element by mass, is retained on Earth by combination with silicon. Aluminum at 8% by mass is more common in the Earth's crust than in the universe and solar system, but the composition of the far more bulky mantle, which has magnesium and iron in place of aluminum (which occurs there only at 2% of mass) more closely mirrors the elemental composition of the solar system, save for the noted loss of volatile elements to space, and loss of iron which has migrated to the Earth's core.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Composition_of_the_human_body" title="Composition of the human body">composition of the human body</a>, by contrast, more closely follows the composition of <a href="/wiki/Seawater" title="Seawater">seawater</a>—save that the human body has additional stores of <a href="/wiki/Carbon" title="Carbon">carbon</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen" title="Nitrogen">nitrogen</a> necessary to form the <a href="/wiki/Protein" title="Protein">proteins</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nucleic_acid" title="Nucleic acid">nucleic acids</a>, together with <a href="/wiki/Phosphorus" title="Phosphorus">phosphorus</a> in the nucleic acids and energy transfer molecule <a href="/wiki/Adenosine_triphosphate" title="Adenosine triphosphate">adenosine triphosphate</a> (ATP) that occurs in the cells of all living organisms. Certain kinds of <a href="/wiki/Organism" title="Organism">organisms</a> require particular additional elements, for example the <a href="/wiki/Magnesium" title="Magnesium">magnesium</a> in <a href="/wiki/Chlorophyll" title="Chlorophyll">chlorophyll</a> in green plants, the <a href="/wiki/Calcium" title="Calcium">calcium</a> in <a href="/wiki/Mollusc_shell" title="Mollusc shell">mollusc shells</a>, or the <a href="/wiki/Iron" title="Iron">iron</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Hemoglobin" title="Hemoglobin">hemoglobin</a> in <a href="/wiki/Vertebrate" title="Vertebrate">vertebrate animals</a>' <a href="/wiki/Red_blood_cell" title="Red blood cell">red blood cells</a>.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:592px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:SolarSystemAbundances.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="357" data-file-width="800" decoding="async" height="263" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/SolarSystemAbundances.png/590px-SolarSystemAbundances.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/e/e6/SolarSystemAbundances.png 1.5x" width="590"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:SolarSystemAbundances.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Abundances of the chemical elements in the Solar System. Hydrogen and helium are most common, from the Big Bang. The next three elements (Li, Be, B) are rare because they are poorly synthesized in the Big Bang and also in stars. The two general trends in the remaining stellar-produced elements are: (1) an alternation of abundance in elements as they have even or odd atomic numbers (the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oddo-Harkins_rule" title="Oddo-Harkins rule">Oddo-Harkins rule</a>), and (2) a general decrease in abundance as elements become heavier. Iron is especially common because it represents the minimum energy nuclide that can be made by fusion of helium in supernovae.</div></div></div>

</div><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#00b000; color:black;"> </span> Quantity elements
