</div><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#cf9; color:black;"> </span> Deemed essential trace element by U.S., not by European Union
</div><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#667f00; color:black;"> </span> Suggested function from deprivation effects or active metabolic handling, but no clearly-identified biochemical function in humans
</div><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#f87b05; color:black;"> </span> Limited circumstantial evidence for trace benefits or biological action in mammals
</div><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#dd9; color:black;"> </span> No evidence for biological action in mammals, but essential in some lower organisms.<br/>(In the case of lanthanum, the definition of an essential nutrient as being indispensable and irreplaceable is not completely applicable due to the extreme similarity of the <a href="/wiki/Lanthanide" title="Lanthanide">lanthanides</a>. The stable early lanthanides up to Sm are known to stimulate the growth of various lanthanide-using organisms.)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup>
</div>
</td></tr></tbody></table></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mendeleev%27s_1869_periodic_table.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="569" data-file-width="512" decoding="async" height="333" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/Mendeleev%27s_1869_periodic_table.svg/300px-Mendeleev%27s_1869_periodic_table.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/Mendeleev%27s_1869_periodic_table.svg/450px-Mendeleev%27s_1869_periodic_table.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/Mendeleev%27s_1869_periodic_table.svg/600px-Mendeleev%27s_1869_periodic_table.svg.png 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mendeleev%27s_1869_periodic_table.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mendeleev" title="Mendeleev">Mendeleev</a>'s 1869 <a href="/wiki/Periodic_table" title="Periodic table">periodic table</a>: <i>An experiment on a system of elements. Based on their atomic weights and chemical similarities.</i></div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Evolving_definitions">Evolving definitions</span></h3>
<p>The concept of an "element" as an undivisible substance has developed through three major historical phases: Classical definitions (such as those of the ancient Greeks), chemical definitions, and atomic definitions.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Classical_definitions">Classical definitions</span></h4>
<p><a href="/wiki/Ancient_philosophy" title="Ancient philosophy">Ancient philosophy</a> posited a set of <a href="/wiki/Classical_element" title="Classical element">classical elements</a> to explain observed patterns in <a href="/wiki/Nature" title="Nature">nature</a>. These <i>elements</i> originally referred to <i><a href="/wiki/Earth_(classical_element)" title="Earth (classical element)">earth</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Water_(classical_element)" title="Water (classical element)">water</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Air_(classical_element)" title="Air (classical element)">air</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Fire_(classical_element)" title="Fire (classical element)">fire</a></i> rather than the chemical elements of modern science.
</p><p>The term 'elements' (<i>stoicheia</i>) was first used by the Greek philosopher <a href="/wiki/Plato" title="Plato">Plato</a> in about 360 BCE in his dialogue <a href="/wiki/Timaeus_(dialogue)" title="Timaeus (dialogue)">Timaeus</a>, which includes a discussion of the composition of inorganic and organic bodies and is a speculative treatise on chemistry. Plato believed the elements introduced a century earlier by <a href="/wiki/Empedocles" title="Empedocles">Empedocles</a> were composed of small <a href="/wiki/Regular_polyhedron" title="Regular polyhedron">polyhedral</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Theory_of_Forms" title="Theory of Forms">forms</a>: <a href="/wiki/Tetrahedron" title="Tetrahedron">tetrahedron</a> (fire), <a href="/wiki/Octahedron" title="Octahedron">octahedron</a> (air), <a href="/wiki/Icosahedron" title="Icosahedron">icosahedron</a> (water), and <a href="/wiki/Cube" title="Cube">cube</a> (earth).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Aristotle" title="Aristotle">Aristotle</a>, c. 350 BCE, also used the term <i>stoicheia</i> and added a fifth element called <a href="/wiki/Aether_(classical_element)" title="Aether (classical element)">aether</a>, which formed the heavens. Aristotle defined an element as:
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r996844942">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Element – one of those bodies into which other bodies can decompose, and that itself is not capable of being divided into other.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Chemical_definitions">Chemical definitions</span></h4>
<p>In 1661, <a href="/wiki/Robert_Boyle" title="Robert Boyle">Robert Boyle</a> proposed his theory of corpuscularism which favoured the analysis of matter as constituted by irreducible units of matter (atoms) and, choosing to side with neither Aristotle's view of the four elements nor <a href="/wiki/Paracelsus" title="Paracelsus">Paracelsus</a>' view of three fundamental elements, left open the question of the number of elements.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-boyle_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-boyle-31">[31]</a></sup> The first modern list of chemical elements was given in <a href="/wiki/Antoine_Lavoisier" title="Antoine Lavoisier">Antoine Lavoisier</a>'s 1789 <i><a href="/wiki/Trait%C3%A9_%C3%89l%C3%A9mentaire_de_Chimie" title="Traité Élémentaire de Chimie">Elements of Chemistry</a></i>, which contained thirty-three elements, including <a href="/wiki/Light" title="Light">light</a> and <a href="/wiki/Caloric_theory" title="Caloric theory">caloric</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup> By 1818, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/J%C3%B6ns_Jakob_Berzelius" title="Jöns Jakob Berzelius">Jöns Jakob Berzelius</a> had determined atomic weights for forty-five of the forty-nine then-accepted elements. <a href="/wiki/Dmitri_Mendeleev" title="Dmitri Mendeleev">Dmitri Mendeleev</a> had sixty-six elements in his <a href="/wiki/Periodic_table" title="Periodic table">periodic table</a> of 1869.
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:DIMendeleevCab.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1593" data-file-width="1134" decoding="async" height="239" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/DIMendeleevCab.jpg/170px-DIMendeleevCab.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/DIMendeleevCab.jpg/255px-DIMendeleevCab.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/DIMendeleevCab.jpg/340px-DIMendeleevCab.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:DIMendeleevCab.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Dmitri_Mendeleev" title="Dmitri Mendeleev">Dmitri Mendeleev</a></div></div></div>
<p>From Boyle until the early 20th century, an element was defined as a pure substance that could not be decomposed into any simpler substance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-boyle_31-1"><a href="#cite_note-boyle-31">[31]</a></sup> Put another way, a chemical element cannot be transformed into other chemical elements by chemical processes. Elements during this time were generally distinguished by their atomic weights, a property measurable with fair accuracy by available analytical techniques.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Atomic_definitions">Atomic definitions</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Henry_Moseley.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="550" data-file-width="369" decoding="async" height="328" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/d/dd/Henry_Moseley.jpg/220px-Henry_Moseley.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/d/dd/Henry_Moseley.jpg/330px-Henry_Moseley.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/d/dd/Henry_Moseley.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Henry_Moseley.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Henry_Moseley" title="Henry Moseley">Henry Moseley</a></div></div></div>
<p>The 1913 discovery by English physicist <a href="/wiki/Henry_Moseley" title="Henry Moseley">Henry Moseley</a> that the nuclear charge is the physical basis for an atom's atomic number, further refined when the nature of protons and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Neutrons" title="Neutrons">neutrons</a> became appreciated, eventually led to the current definition of an element based on atomic number (number of protons per atomic nucleus). The use of atomic numbers, rather than atomic weights, to distinguish elements has greater predictive value (since these numbers are integers), and also resolves some ambiguities in the chemistry-based view due to varying properties of <a href="/wiki/Isotope" title="Isotope">isotopes</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Allotropes" title="Allotropes">allotropes</a> within the same element. Currently, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/IUPAC" title="IUPAC">IUPAC</a> defines an element to exist if it has isotopes with a lifetime longer than the 10<sup>−14</sup> seconds it takes the nucleus to form an electronic cloud.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup>
</p><p>By 1914, seventy-two elements were known, all naturally occurring.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup> The remaining naturally occurring elements were discovered or isolated in subsequent decades, and various additional elements have also been produced synthetically, with much of that work pioneered by <a href="/wiki/Glenn_T._Seaborg" title="Glenn T. Seaborg">Glenn T. Seaborg</a>. In 1955, element 101 was discovered and named <a href="/wiki/Mendelevium" title="Mendelevium">mendelevium</a> in honor of D.I. Mendeleev, the first to arrange the elements in a periodic manner.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Discovery_and_recognition_of_various_elements">Discovery and recognition of various elements</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Timeline_of_chemical_elements_discoveries" title="Timeline of chemical elements discoveries">Timeline of chemical elements discoveries</a></div>
<p>Ten materials familiar to various prehistoric cultures are now known to be chemical elements: <a href="/wiki/Carbon" title="Carbon">Carbon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Copper" title="Copper">copper</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gold" title="Gold">gold</a>, <a href="/wiki/Iron" title="Iron">iron</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lead" title="Lead">lead</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mercury_(element)" title="Mercury (element)">mercury</a>, <a href="/wiki/Silver" title="Silver">silver</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sulfur" title="Sulfur">sulfur</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tin" title="Tin">tin</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Zinc" title="Zinc">zinc</a>. Three additional materials now accepted as elements, <a href="/wiki/Arsenic" title="Arsenic">arsenic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Antimony" title="Antimony">antimony</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Bismuth" title="Bismuth">bismuth</a>, were recognized as distinct substances prior to 1500 AD. <a href="/wiki/Phosphorus" title="Phosphorus">Phosphorus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cobalt" title="Cobalt">cobalt</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Platinum" title="Platinum">platinum</a> were isolated before 1750.
</p><p>Most of the remaining naturally occurring chemical elements were identified and characterized by 1900, including:
</p>
<ul><li>Such now-familiar <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Industry_(manufacturing)" title="Industry (manufacturing)">industrial</a> materials as <a href="/wiki/Aluminium" title="Aluminium">aluminium</a>, <a href="/wiki/Silicon" title="Silicon">silicon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nickel" title="Nickel">nickel</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chromium" title="Chromium">chromium</a>, <a href="/wiki/Magnesium" title="Magnesium">magnesium</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Tungsten" title="Tungsten">tungsten</a></li>
<li>Reactive metals such as <a href="/wiki/Lithium" title="Lithium">lithium</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sodium" title="Sodium">sodium</a>, <a href="/wiki/Potassium" title="Potassium">potassium</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Calcium" title="Calcium">calcium</a></li>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/Halogen" title="Halogen">halogens</a> <a href="/wiki/Fluorine" title="Fluorine">fluorine</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chlorine" title="Chlorine">chlorine</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bromine" title="Bromine">bromine</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Iodine" title="Iodine">iodine</a></li>
<li>Gases such as <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">oxygen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen" title="Nitrogen">nitrogen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Helium" title="Helium">helium</a>, <a href="/wiki/Argon" title="Argon">argon</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Neon" title="Neon">neon</a></li>
<li>Most of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rare-earth_elements" title="Rare-earth elements">rare-earth elements</a>, including <a href="/wiki/Cerium" title="Cerium">cerium</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lanthanum" title="Lanthanum">lanthanum</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gadolinium" title="Gadolinium">gadolinium</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Neodymium" title="Neodymium">neodymium</a>.</li>
<li>The more common <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Radioactive" title="Radioactive">radioactive</a> elements, including <a href="/wiki/Uranium" title="Uranium">uranium</a>, <a href="/wiki/Thorium" title="Thorium">thorium</a>, <a href="/wiki/Radium" title="Radium">radium</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Radon" title="Radon">radon</a></li></ul>
<p>Elements isolated or produced since 1900 include:
</p>
<ul><li>The three remaining undiscovered regularly occurring stable natural elements: <a href="/wiki/Hafnium" title="Hafnium">hafnium</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lutetium" title="Lutetium">lutetium</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Rhenium" title="Rhenium">rhenium</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Plutonium" title="Plutonium">Plutonium</a>, which was first produced synthetically in 1940 by <a href="/wiki/Glenn_T._Seaborg" title="Glenn T. Seaborg">Glenn T. Seaborg</a>, but is now also known from a few long-persisting natural occurrences</li>
<li>The three incidentally occurring natural elements (<a href="/wiki/Neptunium" title="Neptunium">neptunium</a>, <a href="/wiki/Promethium" title="Promethium">promethium</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Technetium" title="Technetium">technetium</a>), which were all first produced synthetically but later discovered in trace amounts in certain geological samples</li>
<li>Four scarce decay products of uranium or thorium (<a href="/wiki/Astatine" title="Astatine">astatine</a>, <a href="/wiki/Francium" title="Francium">francium</a>, <a href="/wiki/Actinium" title="Actinium">actinium</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Protactinium" title="Protactinium">protactinium</a>), and</li>
<li>Various synthetic <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Transuranic" title="Transuranic">transuranic</a> elements, beginning with <a href="/wiki/Americium" title="Americium">americium</a> and <a href="/wiki/Curium" title="Curium">curium</a></li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Recently_discovered_elements">Recently discovered elements</span></h3>
<p>The first <a href="/wiki/Transuranium_element" title="Transuranium element">transuranium element</a> (element with atomic number greater than 92) discovered was <a href="/wiki/Neptunium" title="Neptunium">neptunium</a> in 1940. Since 1999 claims for the discovery of new elements have been considered by the <a href="/wiki/IUPAC/IUPAP_Joint_Working_Party" title="IUPAC/IUPAP Joint Working Party">IUPAC/IUPAP Joint Working Party</a>. As of January 2016, all 118 elements have been confirmed as discovered by <a href="/wiki/International_Union_of_Pure_and_Applied_Chemistry" title="International Union of Pure and Applied Chemistry">IUPAC</a>. The discovery of element 112 was acknowledged in 2009, and the name <i>copernicium</i> and the atomic symbol <i>Cn</i> were suggested for it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> The name and symbol were officially endorsed by IUPAC on 19 February 2010.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> The heaviest element that is believed to have been synthesized to date is element 118, <a href="/wiki/Oganesson" title="Oganesson">oganesson</a>, on 9 October 2006, by the <a href="/wiki/Joint_Institute_for_Nuclear_Research" title="Joint Institute for Nuclear Research">Flerov Laboratory</a> of Nuclear Reactions in <a href="/wiki/Dubna" title="Dubna">Dubna</a>, Russia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schewe_9-1"><a href="#cite_note-Schewe-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Tennessine" title="Tennessine">Tennessine</a>, element 117 was the latest element claimed to be discovered, in 2009.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jinr_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-jinr-38">[38]</a></sup> On 28 November 2016, scientists at the IUPAC officially recognized the names for four of the newest chemical elements, with atomic numbers 113, 115, 117, and 118.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IUPAC-20161130_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-IUPAC-20161130-39">[39]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT-20161201_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-20161201-40">[40]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="List_of_the_118_known_chemical_elements">List of the 118 known chemical elements</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/List_of_chemical_elements" title="List of chemical elements">List of chemical elements</a></div>
<p>The following sortable table shows the 118 known chemical elements.
</p>
<ul><li><b>Atomic number</b>, <b>Element</b>, and <b>Symbol</b> all serve independently as unique identifiers.</li>
<li><b>Element</b> names are those accepted by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/IUPAC" title="IUPAC">IUPAC</a>.</li>
<li><b>Block</b> indicates the periodic table <a href="/wiki/Block_(periodic_table)" title="Block (periodic table)">block</a> for each element: red = s-block, yellow = p-block, blue = d-block, green = f-block.</li>
<li><b>Group</b> and <b>period</b> refer to an element's position in the <a href="/wiki/Periodic_table" title="Periodic table">periodic table</a>. Group numbers here show the currently accepted numbering; for older numberings, see <a href="/wiki/Group_(periodic_table)" title="Group (periodic table)">Group (periodic table)</a>.</li></ul>
